    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1777551966" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1777551966" />
    <a title="Maria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57090347-maria"><img alt="Maria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1474188682p2/57090347.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1777551966?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria" href="/user/show/57090347-maria">Maria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780751547870" href="/book/show/13101889-memoirs-of-an-imaginary-friend">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1777551966" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3306734224818434453"><br/>Spoiler alert: I have not hidden any parts of this review, and although I only write in general terms about the book's ending, this might constitute a spoiler for some.<br/><br/>Budo is the imaginary friend of Max, an 8 year old kid who's somewhere on the aspergers/autism scale, but not diagnosed yet. Budo is Max's creation, and exists only as long as Max believes in him. Although the idea of telling a story from the perspective of an imaginary friend is cute and refreshing, I am sad to say that I tired o</span>
  <span id="freeText3306734224818434453" style="display:none"><br />Spoiler alert: I have not hidden any parts of this review, and although I only write in general terms about the book's ending, this might constitute a spoiler for some.<br /><br />Budo is the imaginary friend of Max, an 8 year old kid who's somewhere on the aspergers/autism scale, but not diagnosed yet. Budo is Max's creation, and exists only as long as Max believes in him. Although the idea of telling a story from the perspective of an imaginary friend is cute and refreshing, I am sad to say that I tired of this book well before the end. <br /><br />As a character, Budo is a weird mix of child and adult. His language is very childlike and simple, which makes the book a quick read. But Budo is also wise beyond his years, and the book is filled to the brim with something I, for the lack of a better term (but I'm sure one exists), will call "child-wisdom". When a child's simple, naive view of the world results in deep, philosophical thoughts about life, love, death, friendship, loyalty and so on. And this worked well for a while, but there was just too much of it.<br /><br />I liked how Budo explained about the life of an imaginary friend. I liked his conversations with other imaginary friends. And I really liked the painful psychology of these creatures - to be at the mercy of the kids that created them, the kids they love and want to protect, and at the same time the egocentric, but completely understandable, need for a life of their own. <br /><br />There seemed to be a lot of potential in a set up like that. <br /><br />What the book ends up doing with this premise is rather disappointing though, and the ending was, at least for me, a predictable, emotional cliché.<br /><br />One of the books strengths is that it introduced us to a child with aspergers/autism, and explains how such a child sees the world. This reminded me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1618.The_Curious_Incident_of_the_Dog_in_the_Night_Time?ac=1&amp;from_search=true" rel="nofollow noopener">The curious Incident of the Dog in the Night-Time</a> by Mark Haddon. But where Haddon thoroughly explains how horrible it is for his main character to be out in the big city on his own, Matthew Dicks' Max seems to grow with the challenges he is presented. All he needed was a little push. <br /><br />Your base personality is something that changes very little throughout your life, and it certainly doesn't change just because you really need it to. In difficult situations, we do not suddenly cope better than we ordinarily would, to the contrary, we usually do worse. People that suddenly find their courage just when they need it, prompted by circumstances to overcome some personal fear, is something of a pet peeve for me. I can see the author pulling the strings every time this happens. It is a very common plot twist, and it makes for a very predictable ending.<br /><br />I did not like the way Budo's story ended either, giving in to the unfair premise of his existence, rather than fighting for a life of his own. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3306734224818434453" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer334364958" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3212225653304961360">As a child I never had an imaginary friend, and having read Budo's and Max's story, I now feel as though I missed out big time. Their relationship is so special, so heart-warming and virtually irreplaceable by a friendship amongst normal mortals. <br/><br/>As if getting into the hearts and minds of both Budo and Max wasn't brillantly enough, Matthew Dicks then takes us on a very suspenseful adventure, and for a couple of hundred pages I was inseparable from the book (which incidentally was great news for </span>
  <span id="freeText3212225653304961360" style="display:none">As a child I never had an imaginary friend, and having read Budo's and Max's story, I now feel as though I missed out big time. Their relationship is so special, so heart-warming and virtually irreplaceable by a friendship amongst normal mortals. <br /><br />As if getting into the hearts and minds of both Budo and Max wasn't brillantly enough, Matthew Dicks then takes us on a very suspenseful adventure, and for a couple of hundred pages I was inseparable from the book (which incidentally was great news for my fitness as I only ever use the e-book reader at the gym). <br /><br />The only question mark this book had for me is that I couldn't quite work out who the target readership is supposed to be. Subject matter, style and language (with the exception of a few strong expletives) all point to a middle-grade readership, but I did spot the book in the adults section in a Berlin bookshop. Odd? <br /><br />Wonderfully drawn characters, a gripping plot and the combination of many laugh-out-loud as well as tearful moments make this story a real winner "in my books"! Now I just have to subtly manipulate my book club pals into voting it from a shortlist of three for our next selection! <br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_401817351" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/401817351" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1179650-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1405201179p2/1179650.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/401817351?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/1179650-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer401817351" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer773578963161061302">Reading this book made me feel like a child again. I loved Budo. I wished that I had such a vivid imagination as Max did with Budo when I was growing up. I never really had an imaginary friend growing up but this probably had to do with I had a younger sister to play with. She and I would make things up ourselves. However, if I was an only child, I would have wanted a friend like Budo. It was very creative of Max to give Budo the ability to walk through walls. <br/><br/>I do admit that at first I was a li</span>
  <span id="freeText773578963161061302" style="display:none">Reading this book made me feel like a child again. I loved Budo. I wished that I had such a vivid imagination as Max did with Budo when I was growing up. I never really had an imaginary friend growing up but this probably had to do with I had a younger sister to play with. She and I would make things up ourselves. However, if I was an only child, I would have wanted a friend like Budo. It was very creative of Max to give Budo the ability to walk through walls. <br /><br />I do admit that at first I was a little leery as I was not sure where the story was going to lead and wondered how Mr. Dicks was going to put together a whole book about an imaginary person. I should not have worried. This book was an instant hit with me. I was hooked. Max and Budo stole the show. I have to agree with another reader that I did flash forward some in the book but not because I was bored but because I wanted to see what was going to happen next. I wanted to make that leap sooner. The ending was both good and sad. I won’t get into too much detail as I don’t want to give anything away. Memoirs of an Imaginary Friend is an A+! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="773578963161061302" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_313304772" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/313304772" />
    <a title="Tania" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6450088-tania"><img alt="Tania" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603638299p2/6450088.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/313304772?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tania" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tania" href="/user/show/6450088-tania">Tania</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780751547870" href="/book/show/13101889-memoirs-of-an-imaginary-friend">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6450088-tania?shelf=favorite-books">favorite-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6450088-tania?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer313304772" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5524626298352343599">This was such a sweet, innocent story. Told from the viewpoint of an imaginary friend of a boy with aspegers, it is very original. It made me think about imaginary friends in a differant way. It also made me cry. I would recommend it to anyone who enjoyed room or curious incident of a dog at nighttime.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1655441193" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1655441193" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3498889-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590863781p2/3498889.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1655441193?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/3498889-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3498889-cheryl?shelf=to-enjoy-again">to-enjoy-again</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1655441193" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer406561618543444502">Bravo. So original, so refreshing. Intense, but the yuck factor wasn't as high as I feared, and would probably not bother most readers, even 12 year-olds, at all. I could pick a few nits, but I won't, because they're really minor compared to the joy of the story as a whole. I will definitely look for more by the author.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('S0JnmHx6CVhebBKSpo8GMiicscLF+/4nuEeANrgyTvYFfeTgKVku5vrMTjvedLv1zfhPrm1MSHSZ8SfEaDuv/g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mlQSYY/J4Nbb1T/zGMXj3o20XJkEKcwHjsolEbCL5TrUa5EZ2urHaH91Y1pgPl4ZaNCi9ayeelSvfILjYIIEMg==')})
  }
//]]>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10143034059205639207">Matthew Dicks is the internationally bestselling author of the novels Memoirs of an Imaginary Friend, Something Missing, Unexpectedly, Milo, The Perfect Comeback of Caroline Jacobs, and the upcoming novels The Other Mother and Cardboard Knight, as well as the nonfiction Storyworthy: Engage, Teach, Persuade, and Change Your Life Through the Art of Storytelling. His novels have been translated into </span>
  <span id="freeText10143034059205639207" style="display:none">Matthew Dicks is the internationally bestselling author of the novels Memoirs of an Imaginary Friend, Something Missing, Unexpectedly, Milo, The Perfect Comeback of Caroline Jacobs, and the upcoming novels The Other Mother and Cardboard Knight, as well as the nonfiction Storyworthy: Engage, Teach, Persuade, and Change Your Life Through the Art of Storytelling. His novels have been translated into more than 25 languages worldwide. <br /><br />Memoirs of an Imaginary Friend was the 2014 Dolly Gray Award winner and was nominated for a 2017 Nutmeg Award in Connecticut. Matthew was also awarded first prize in 2016 and second prize in 2017 in the Magazine/Humorous Column category by the CT Society of Professional Journalists. <br /><br />He is also the author of the rock opera The Clowns and the musicals Caught in the Middle, Sticks &amp; Stones, and Summertime. He has written comic books for Double Take comics. He is a columnist for Seasons magazine and has published work in Reader's Digest, The Hartford Courant, Parents magazine, The Huffington Post, and The Christian Science Monitor. He was awarded first prize for opinion writing in 2015 by the Connecticut Society of Professional Journalists. <br /><br />When not hunched over a computer screen, Matthew fills his days as an elementary school teacher, a storyteller, a speaking coach, a blogger, a wedding DJ, a minister, a life coach, and a Lord of Sealand. He has been teaching for 20 years and is a former West Hartford Teacher of the Year and a finalist for Connecticut Teacher of the Year.<br /><br />Matthew is a 35-time Moth StorySLAM champion and 5-time GrandSLAM champion whose stories have been featured on their nationally syndicated Moth Radio Hour and their weekly podcast. He has also told stories for This American Life, TED, The Colin McEnroe Show, The Story Collider, The Liar Show, Literary Death Match, The Mouth, and many others. He has performed in such venues as the Brooklyn Academy of Music, The Wilbur Theater, The Academy of Music in North Hampton, CT, The Bynam Theater of Pittsburgh, The Bell House in NYC, The Lebanon Opera House, Boston University, and Infinity Hall in Hartford, CT.<br /><br />He is a regular guest on several Slate podcasts, including The Gist, where he teaches storytelling.<br /><br />Matthew is also the co-founder and creative director of Speak Up, a Hartford-based storytelling organization that produces shows throughout New England. He teaches storytelling and public speaking to individuals, corporations, and school districts around the world. He has most recently taught at Yale University, The University of Connecticut Law School, Purdue University, The Connecticut Historical Society, Kripalu Center for Yoga and Health, Miss Porter's School, The Berkshire School, and Graded School in Sao Paulo, Brazil. <br /><br />Matthew is the creator and co-host of Boy vs. Girl, a podcast about gender and gender stereotypes. <br /><br />Matthew is married to friend and fellow teacher, Elysha, and they have two children, Clara and Charlie. He grew up in the small town of Blackstone, Massachusetts, where he made a name for himself by dying twice before the age of eighteen and becoming the first student in his high school to be suspended for inciting riot upon himself. </span>
